## Chapter 8: GPS Signal Structure and Modulation:




### Section: 8.1 Understanding GPS Signal Structure

The Global Positioning System (GPS) is a satellite-based navigation system that provides precise location and time information to GPS-enabled devices. In this section, we will explore the basic principles of GPS signals, including their frequency, bandwidth, and modulation techniques.

#### 8.1a Basics of GPS Signal Structure

The GPS signal structure is designed to transmit a variety of information, including satellite positions, the state of the internal clocks, and the health of the network. These signals are transmitted on two separate carrier frequencies, L1 and L2, which are common to all satellites in the network. The L1 frequency is used for civilian applications, while the L2 frequency is reserved for military use.

The GPS signal structure is encoded using two different encodings: a public encoding and an encrypted encoding. The public encoding enables lower resolution navigation, while the encrypted encoding is used by the U.S. military for more precise navigation.

The message format of the GPS signal structure is divided into five subframes, each 300 bits long. The first subframe encodes the week number and the time within the week, as well as the health of the satellite. The second and third subframes contain the "ephemeris" - the precise orbit for the satellite. The fourth and fifth subframes contain the "almanac", which contains coarse orbit and status information for up to 32 satellites in the constellation.

The GPS signal structure is transmitted at a rate of 50 bits per second, and each complete message takes 750 seconds to complete. This gives a total of 37,500 bits per message. The transmission of the message is precisely timed, with each 30-second frame beginning on the minute or half-minute as indicated by the atomic clock on each satellite.

The GPS signal structure is designed to be robust and reliable, with error correction capabilities built into the message format. This ensures that even in the event of signal interference or degradation, the receiver can still obtain an accurate satellite location.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the different types of GPS signals and their respective uses. We will also explore the various modulation techniques used in GPS signals, such as binary offset carrier (BOC) modulation and spread spectrum modulation. 


## Chapter 8: GPS Signal Structure and Modulation




### Related Context
```
# Urban canyon

### GPS signal reception

When using GPS receivers in street canyons with tall buildings, the shadowing and multipath effects may contribute to poor GPS signal reception # Multiplexed binary offset carrier

Multiplexed binary offset carrier (MBOC) modulation is a modulation design proposed for Galileo and modernized GPS satellite navigation signals, which combines a sine binary offset carrier SinBOC(1,1) signal with a SinBOC(6,1) signal, either via weighted sum/difference (the CBOC implementation) or via time-multiplexing (the TMBOC implementation).

The main objective of the common GPS and Galileo signal design activity was that the power spectral density (PSD) of the proposed solution would be identical for GPS L1C and Galileo E1 OS signals when the pilot and data components are computed together. This assures high interoperability between both signals. Its power spectral density <math>\Phi(f)</math> is defined by

MBOC has two main implementations, the Composite Binary Offset Carrier (CBOC) implementation, currently used in Galileo, and the Time-Multiplexed Binary Offset Carrier (TMBOC), currently used in the modernized GPS L1C signal # GPS signals

### Navigation message

In addition to the PRN ranging codes, a receiver needs to know the time and position of each active satellite. GPS encodes this information into the "navigation message" and modulates it onto both the C/A and P(Y) ranging codes at 50 bit/s. The navigation message format described in this section is called LNAV data (for "legacy navigation").

The navigation message conveys information of three types:

An ephemeris is valid for only four hours; an almanac is valid with little dilution of precision for up to two weeks. The receiver uses the almanac to acquire a set of satellites based on stored time and location. As each satellite is acquired, its ephemeris is decoded so the satellite can be used for navigation.

The navigation message consists of 30-second "frames" 1,500 bits long. Each frame contains 30-bit words, with the first 10 bits representing the frame number, the next 10 bits representing the subframe number, and the remaining 10 bits representing the message type. The message type can be either 0 (for ephemeris data) or 1 (for almanac data).

The ephemeris data includes the satellite's position, velocity, and time information, while the almanac data includes information about the satellite's status and health. The navigation message also includes a checksum to ensure the integrity of the transmitted data.

The navigation message is transmitted on both the L1 and L2 frequencies, with the L1 frequency being used for civilian applications and the L2 frequency being used for military applications. The navigation message is modulated onto the ranging codes using a technique called "spread spectrum" modulation, which allows for the transmission of multiple signals simultaneously on the same frequency band.

The navigation message is transmitted continuously, with each frame being repeated every 30 seconds. This allows for continuous monitoring of the satellite's position and status, ensuring accurate navigation.

In conclusion, the navigation message is a crucial component of the GPS signal structure, providing the necessary information for accurate navigation. Its structure and modulation techniques are carefully designed to ensure robustness and reliability, making it an essential part of the GPS system.





### Subsection: 8.1c GPS Signal Structure in Practice

In the previous section, we discussed the theoretical aspects of GPS signal structure and modulation. In this section, we will explore how these concepts are applied in practice.

#### 8.1c.1 Multiplexed Binary Offset Carrier (MBOC) Modulation

As mentioned in the previous section, MBOC modulation is a modulation design proposed for Galileo and modernized GPS satellite navigation signals. It combines a sine binary offset carrier SinBOC(1,1) signal with a SinBOC(6,1) signal, either via weighted sum/difference (the CBOC implementation) or via time-multiplexing (the TMBOC implementation).

The main objective of the common GPS and Galileo signal design activity was that the power spectral density (PSD) of the proposed solution would be identical for GPS L1C and Galileo E1 OS signals when the pilot and data components are computed together. This assures high interoperability between both signals. Its power spectral density $\Phi(f)$ is defined by

$$
\Phi(f) = \frac{1}{2} \left( \Phi_{SinBOC(1,1)}(f) + \Phi_{SinBOC(6,1)}(f) \right)
$$

where $\Phi_{SinBOC(1,1)}(f)$ and $\Phi_{SinBOC(6,1)}(f)$ are the power spectral densities of the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals, respectively.

MBOC has two main implementations, the Composite Binary Offset Carrier (CBOC) implementation, currently used in Galileo, and the Time-Multiplexed Binary Offset Carrier (TMBOC), currently used in the modernized GPS L1C signal.

#### 8.1c.2 GPS Signal Reception in Urban Canyons

In urban areas, the presence of tall buildings can create what is known as an "urban canyon", which can significantly affect GPS signal reception. The shadowing and multipath effects in these canyons can lead to poor GPS signal reception, making it difficult for receivers to accurately determine their position.

To mitigate these effects, GPS receivers often use techniques such as signal processing and error correction to improve the accuracy of their position estimates. These techniques can help to compensate for the effects of shadowing and multipath propagation, allowing the receiver to obtain a more accurate position estimate.

#### 8.1c.3 GPS Signal Modulation in Practice

In practice, GPS signal modulation involves the encoding of navigation data onto the GPS signal. This data includes information about the satellite's position, velocity, and time, as well as other navigation data.

The navigation message, as described in the previous section, is used to convey this information. It consists of 30-second "frames" 1,500 bits long, divided into five 6-second "subframes" of ten 30-bit words each. Each subframe has the GPS time in 6-second increments.

The navigation message is modulated onto both the C/A and P(Y) ranging codes at 50 bit/s. This allows the receiver to decode the navigation data and use it to determine its position.

In conclusion, understanding the practical aspects of GPS signal structure and modulation is crucial for anyone working with GPS technology. It allows us to design and implement effective signal processing techniques to improve the accuracy of GPS position estimates, even in challenging environments such as urban canyons.




### Subsection: 8.2a Basics of GPS Signal Modulation

GPS signal modulation is a critical aspect of the Global Positioning System. It involves the process of modifying a carrier signal to carry information. In the context of GPS, the carrier signal is the electromagnetic wave transmitted by the satellite, and the information it carries includes the satellite's position, velocity, and time.

#### 8.2a.1 Types of GPS Signal Modulation

There are two main types of GPS signal modulation: frequency modulation (FM) and phase modulation (PM). FM is used for the L1 frequency band, while PM is used for the L2 frequency band. Both types of modulation are used to encode the navigation message, which is a series of bits that contain the satellite's position, velocity, and time information.

#### 8.2a.2 Frequency Modulation (FM)

Frequency modulation is a type of modulation where the frequency of the carrier signal is varied in proportion to the amplitude of the modulating signal. In the context of GPS, the modulating signal is the navigation message. The navigation message is first converted into a series of pulses, each of which corresponds to a bit in the message. These pulses are then used to vary the frequency of the carrier signal.

The frequency of the carrier signal is varied by changing the phase of the signal. This is achieved by using a technique known as phase modulation (PM). The phase of the carrier signal is changed by an amount proportional to the amplitude of the modulating signal. This results in a frequency modulated signal.

#### 8.2a.3 Phase Modulation (PM)

Phase modulation is a type of modulation where the phase of the carrier signal is varied in proportion to the amplitude of the modulating signal. In the context of GPS, the modulating signal is the navigation message. The navigation message is first converted into a series of pulses, each of which corresponds to a bit in the message. These pulses are then used to vary the phase of the carrier signal.

The phase of the carrier signal is varied by changing the frequency of the signal. This is achieved by using a technique known as frequency modulation (FM). The frequency of the carrier signal is changed by an amount proportional to the amplitude of the modulating signal. This results in a phase modulated signal.

#### 8.2a.4 Modulation Index

The modulation index is a measure of the amount of modulation applied to the carrier signal. It is defined as the ratio of the maximum frequency deviation to the maximum frequency of the carrier signal. The modulation index is a critical parameter in GPS signal modulation as it determines the bandwidth of the modulated signal.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the specifics of GPS signal modulation, discussing the various techniques used to encode the navigation message and the challenges faced in implementing these techniques.




### Subsection: 8.2b Advanced GPS Signal Modulation Techniques

In addition to the basic modulation techniques of frequency modulation (FM) and phase modulation (PM), advanced GPS signal modulation techniques have been developed to improve the performance of the Global Positioning System. These techniques include Multiplexed Binary Offset Carrier (MBOC) modulation and Composite Binary Offset Carrier (CBOC) modulation.

#### 8.2b.1 Multiplexed Binary Offset Carrier (MBOC) Modulation

Multiplexed Binary Offset Carrier (MBOC) modulation is a modulation design proposed for Galileo and modernized GPS satellite navigation signals. It combines a sine binary offset carrier SinBOC(1,1) signal with a SinBOC(6,1) signal, either via weighted sum/difference (the CBOC implementation) or via time-multiplexing (the TMBOC implementation).

The main objective of the common GPS and Galileo signal design activity was that the power spectral density (PSD) of the proposed solution would be identical for GPS L1C and Galileo E1 OS signals when the pilot and data components are computed together. This assures high interoperability between both signals. Its power spectral density $\Phi(f)$ is defined by

$$
\Phi(f) = \frac{1}{2} \left[ \Phi_{\text{SinBOC(1,1)}}(f) + \Phi_{\text{SinBOC(6,1)}}(f) \right]
$$

where $\Phi_{\text{SinBOC(1,1)}}(f)$ and $\Phi_{\text{SinBOC(6,1)}}(f)$ are the power spectral densities of the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals, respectively.

MBOC has two main implementations, the Composite Binary Offset Carrier (CBOC) implementation, currently used in Galileo, and the Time-Multiplexed Binary Offset Carrier (TMBOC), currently used in the modernized GPS L1C signal.

#### 8.2b.2 Composite Binary Offset Carrier (CBOC) Modulation

Composite Binary Offset Carrier (CBOC) modulation is a type of MBOC modulation where the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals are combined via weighted sum/difference. This implementation is currently used in Galileo.

The CBOC implementation of MBOC modulation can be represented as

$$
s(t) = \sqrt{\frac{2}{T}} \left[ A_1 \cos(2\pi f_1 t + \phi_1) + A_6 \cos(2\pi f_6 t + \phi_6) \right]
$$

where $s(t)$ is the composite signal, $A_1$ and $A_6$ are the amplitudes of the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals, respectively, $f_1$ and $f_6$ are the carrier frequencies of the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals, respectively, and $\phi_1$ and $\phi_6$ are the phases of the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals, respectively.

The CBOC implementation of MBOC modulation has the advantage of simplicity, but it also has some disadvantages. For example, it can cause interference with other signals in the frequency band, and it can lead to a higher level of noise in the receiver.

#### 8.2b.3 Time-Multiplexed Binary Offset Carrier (TMBOC) Modulation

Time-Multiplexed Binary Offset Carrier (TMBOC) modulation is a type of MBOC modulation where the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals are combined via time-multiplexing. This implementation is currently used in the modernized GPS L1C signal.

The TMBOC implementation of MBOC modulation can be represented as

$$
s(t) = \sqrt{\frac{2}{T}} \left[ A_1 \cos(2\pi f_1 t + \phi_1) + A_6 \cos(2\pi f_6 t + \phi_6) \right]
$$

where $s(t)$ is the composite signal, $A_1$ and $A_6$ are the amplitudes of the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals, respectively, $f_1$ and $f_6$ are the carrier frequencies of the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals, respectively, and $\phi_1$ and $\phi_6$ are the phases of the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals, respectively.

The TMBOC implementation of MBOC modulation has the advantage of improved spectral efficiency compared to the CBOC implementation, but it also has some disadvantages. For example, it can cause more complex receiver design, and it can lead to a higher level of noise in the receiver.




### Subsection: 8.2c GPS Signal Modulation in Practice

In practice, the modulation of GPS signals is a complex process that involves the application of various techniques to ensure the efficient transmission and reception of signals. The modulation techniques used in GPS signals are designed to optimize the use of the available bandwidth and to minimize interference from other signals.

#### 8.2c.1 Frequency Modulation (FM)

Frequency modulation (FM) is a type of modulation where the frequency of a carrier signal is varied in accordance with the amplitude of the modulating signal. In GPS signals, FM is used to modulate the carrier signal with the navigation message. The navigation message is a series of bits that contain information about the satellite's position, velocity, and time. The FM modulation of the navigation message allows for the efficient transmission of this information over the air interface.

The FM modulation of the navigation message is achieved by varying the frequency of the carrier signal in accordance with the bits of the navigation message. This is done by changing the instantaneous frequency of the carrier signal. The change in frequency is proportional to the change in the bits of the navigation message. This results in a modulated signal that contains the navigation message.

#### 8.2c.2 Phase Modulation (PM)

Phase modulation (PM) is a type of modulation where the phase of a carrier signal is varied in accordance with the amplitude of the modulating signal. In GPS signals, PM is used to modulate the carrier signal with the navigation message. The navigation message is a series of bits that contain information about the satellite's position, velocity, and time. The PM modulation of the navigation message allows for the efficient transmission of this information over the air interface.

The PM modulation of the navigation message is achieved by varying the phase of the carrier signal in accordance with the bits of the navigation message. This is done by changing the instantaneous phase of the carrier signal. The change in phase is proportional to the change in the bits of the navigation message. This results in a modulated signal that contains the navigation message.

#### 8.2c.3 Multiplexed Binary Offset Carrier (MBOC) Modulation

Multiplexed Binary Offset Carrier (MBOC) modulation is a modulation design proposed for Galileo and modernized GPS satellite navigation signals. It combines a sine binary offset carrier SinBOC(1,1) signal with a SinBOC(6,1) signal, either via weighted sum/difference (the CBOC implementation) or via time-multiplexing (the TMBOC implementation).

The main objective of the common GPS and Galileo signal design activity was that the power spectral density (PSD) of the proposed solution would be identical for GPS L1C and Galileo E1 OS signals when the pilot and data components are computed together. This assures high interoperability between both signals. Its power spectral density $\Phi(f)$ is defined by

$$
\Phi(f) = \frac{1}{2} \left[ \Phi_{\text{SinBOC(1,1)}}(f) + \Phi_{\text{SinBOC(6,1)}}(f) \right]
$$

where $\Phi_{\text{SinBOC(1,1)}}(f)$ and $\Phi_{\text{SinBOC(6,1)}}(f)$ are the power spectral densities of the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals, respectively.

MBOC has two main implementations, the Composite Binary Offset Carrier (CBOC) implementation, currently used in Galileo, and the Time-Multiplexed Binary Offset Carrier (TMBOC), currently used in the modernized GPS L1C signal.

#### 8.2c.4 Composite Binary Offset Carrier (CBOC) Modulation

Composite Binary Offset Carrier (CBOC) modulation is a type of MBOC modulation where the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals are combined via weighted sum/difference. This implementation is currently used in Galileo.

The CBOC implementation of MBOC modulation is achieved by combining the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals via a weighted sum/difference. The weights are determined by the power spectral density of the signals. The resulting signal is a composite signal that contains both the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals.

The CBOC implementation of MBOC modulation is designed to optimize the use of the available bandwidth and to minimize interference from other signals. It achieves this by combining the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals in a way that results in a signal with a power spectral density that is identical to that of the GPS L1C signal. This ensures high interoperability between both signals.

#### 8.2c.5 Time-Multiplexed Binary Offset Carrier (TMBOC) Modulation

Time-Multiplexed Binary Offset Carrier (TMBOC) modulation is another type of MBOC modulation where the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals are combined via time-multiplexing. This implementation is currently used in the modernized GPS L1C signal.

The TMBOC implementation of MBOC modulation is achieved by time-multiplexing the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals. This is done by alternating between the two signals in time. The resulting signal is a time-multiplexed signal that contains both the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals.

The TMBOC implementation of MBOC modulation is designed to optimize the use of the available bandwidth and to minimize interference from other signals. It achieves this by combining the SinBOC(1,1) and SinBOC(6,1) signals in a way that results in a signal with a power spectral density that is identical to that of the GPS L1C signal. This ensures high interoperability between both signals.
